    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="99aa962964e84c27b3929295426ae25f">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '393f12bf-5e3e-41c7-8101-89f230568bf3'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

China</p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Chen/Bingbing">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span><span class="accordion-tabbed__tab-mobile  accordion__closed"><a href="/authored-by/Yang/Hui+Ying" class="author-name accordion-tabbed__control" data-id="a6" data-db-target-for="a6" aria-controls="a6" aria-haspopup="true" id="a6_Ctrl" role="button"><span>Hui Ying Yang</span><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-section_arrow_d"></i></a><span class="comma-separator">,&nbsp;</span><div class="author-info accordion-tabbed__content" data-db-target-of="a6" aria-labelledby="a6_Ctrl" role="region" id="a6">
                     <p class="author-name">Hui Ying Yang</p>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d174338191" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>NASICON-type cathode with remarkable ionic conductivity is perspective candidate for fast-charging sodium-ion battery. However, severely restricted by low electrical conductivity and poor interfacial kinetics, it usually delivers poor charge transfer kinetics. Different from traditional carbon compositing with high carbon contents, herein, a trace carbon incorporation tactic is proposed based on a typical NASICON-structured Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>. First, particle-growth process of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> is regulated via incorporating carbon dot, significantly reducing its particle size to shorten charge diffusion path. Second, electrical conductivity of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> is improved without sacrificing its high electrochemical activity due to the incorporated trace carbon content (0.76&nbsp;wt.%). Third, Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>-electrolyte interface structure is optimized by abundant functional groups from the incorporated carbon dot, enabling a thin and stable NaF-rich CEI layer to boost interface kinetics. As a result, carbon dot endows Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> with ultrastable cyclability up to 20&nbsp;k cycles (capacity retention of 98.4%) and excellent rate capability (up to 200&nbsp;C) in half cell, as well as high energy density (368.7&nbsp;Wh&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup>) and fast charging property (≈110.2&nbsp;s per charging with 250.8&nbsp;Wh&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup> input) in full cell. This study carves a new path for developing fast-charging cathode, as is increasingly desired for present energy storage applications.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202400595-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202400595-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>High energy density and fast charging feature are highly desirable for sodium-ion battery (SIB) to push its application in electric vehicles and portable electronics.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400595-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400595-bib-0001_R_d174338181e534" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Nature">1</a>-<a href="#aenm202400595-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400595-bib-0005_R_d174338181e534" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Mater. Today">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Electrode material plays a decisive impact in determining how fast SIB can be charged.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400595-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400595-bib-0006_R_d174338181e539" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Adv. Energy Mater.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> So far, remarkable progress has been made in researching fast-charging anode materials, such as hard carbons and metal compounds.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400595-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400595-bib-0007_R_d174338181e544" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA">7</a>-<a href="#aenm202400595-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400595-bib-0014_R_d174338181e544" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Mater.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, developing superior cathode material with fast charging feature and high energy density of SIB remains challenging. Among various cathode materials, polyanions have received much attention due to their smaller structure change during (de)sodiation in comparison to layered materials, and higher operating potential versus Prussian blue analogs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400595-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400595-bib-0015_R_d174338181e550" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Adv. Energy Mater.">15</a>-<a href="#aenm202400595-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400595-bib-0018_R_d174338181e550" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Chem. Sci.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Remarkably, NASICON structured polyanion such as Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NVP) with superior ion conductivity (10<sup>−9</sup> to 10<sup>−11</sup>&nbsp;cm<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>) has been proved to be appealing for fast-charging SIB.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400595-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400595-bib-0019_R_d174338181e573" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Adv. Energy Mater.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Nevertheless, its charge transfer kinetics is greatly limited owing to the low electrical conductivity and slow interfacial kinetics,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400595-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400595-bib-0020_R_d174338181e578" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">20</a>, <a href="#aenm202400595-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400595-bib-0021_R_d174338181e578" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Carbon Energy">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> inevitably resulting in its poor electrochemical performances.</p>
         
         <p>Recently, a series of valid tactics have been employed to overcome the challenges, such as carbon composting and structure engineering.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400595-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400595-bib-0020_R_d174338181e586" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For example, Qiao's group found that sodium-ion diffusion path of NVP could be well tuned via designing abundant meso/macropores (specific surface area of 104&nbsp;m<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) as well as incorporating carbon (content of 4.40&nbsp;wt.%) based on a polymer stabilized droplet template strategy, thus realizing excellent rate (up to 100&nbsp;C) and cycling performances (0.005% capacity fading per cycle during 10&nbsp;k cycles).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400595-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400595-bib-0022_R_d174338181e595" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Liang et&nbsp;al. improved the charge transfer kinetics of NVP through combining Mo doping with carbon coating (content of 4.98&nbsp;wt.%) and nanowire designing based on an electrospinning approach, resulting in its excellent rate capability up to 150&nbsp;C and high capacity retention of 95.3% after 2000 cycles. Moreover, a high energy density of 264.2&nbsp;Wh&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup> could be delivered when coupling with hard carbon anode in full cell.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400595-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400595-bib-0023_R_d174338181e603" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Adv. Energy Mater.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Pan's group investigated that carbon dot incorporation could prompt morphology evolution of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>3</sub>, in favor of realizing high rate performance (84.7&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 50&nbsp;C), excellent cycling performances (capacity retention of 90.2% after 6000 cycles), and an energy density of 159&nbsp;Wh&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup> under a power density of 64&nbsp;W&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup> in full cell.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400595-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400595-bib-0024_R_d174338181e626" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - J. Mater. Chem. A">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Despite these remarkable progresses, some inadequacies and challenges remain: i) Carbon compositing is a most common strategy to improve charge transfer kinetics of NVP. However, high carbon contents (&gt;4&nbsp;wt.%) were usually employed in the traditional carbon compositing strategy, and electrochemical activity of NVP will be inevitably reduced due to poor electrochemical activity of carbon at high operating potential. Therefore, it is challenging to balance the competitive relation between electrical conductivity and electrochemical activity of NVP. ii) Nanostructure engineering can shorten ion-diffusion path to accelerate diffusive sodium-ion transport across the NVP. Nevertheless, the induced high specific surface area may result in many interfacial side reactions with large irreversible capacity as well as lower compaction density of electrodes. Moreover, nanostructure design mostly relies on complex synthesis processes, which will be inconducive to large-scale production. iii) Kinetics and electrochemical performances of NASICON structured polyanion could be improved via incorporating carbon dot.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400595-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400595-bib-0024_R_d174338181e631" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - J. Mater. Chem. A">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Nevertheless, effective design of the carbon dot structure as well as in-depth understanding of relation between the carbon dot and NASICON structured polyanion are still highly desired, aiming at realizing high energy density and fast charging feature of NVP. iv) It is well known that cathode-electrolyte interface (CEI) structure, plays a critical role in promoting charge transfer kinetics and structure evolution of cathodes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400595-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400595-bib-0025_R_d174338181e636" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Carbon Neutrality">25</a>, <a href="#aenm202400595-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400595-bib-0026_R_d174338181e636" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Nano Res.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, construction of high-quality CEI layer on NVP cathode aiming at the fast charging capability and high energy density is seldom reported, and comprehensive understanding behind the interface structure engineering on NVP remain unexplored.</p>
         
         <p>Herein, a feasible carbon dot incorporation strategy is employed to address the above challenges, with the purpose of unlocking charge transfer limitation in NVP. The optimization mechanism is illustrated in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400595-fig-0001">1a</a>. First, carbon dot can be served as ionic surfactant to regulate particle-growth process of NVP.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400595-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400595-bib-0027_R_d174338181e650" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - J. Mater. Chem. C">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Thus, the incorporated carbon dot with abundant surface functional groups (─OH and ─NH<sub>2</sub>) and ζ potential of 4.54&nbsp;mV can be tightly linked to NVP surface, effectively restraining further growth of NVP crystals (specific surface area is slightly increased from 2.5 to 17.5&nbsp;m<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>). This can shorten ion-diffusion path to accelerate diffusion kinetics. Then, in comparison to traditional carbon compositing with high carbon contents, electrical conductivity of NVP is significantly boosted via trace carbon incorporation (content of 0.76&nbsp;wt.%), well balancing the competitive relation between electrical conductivity and electrochemical activity of NVP. Last, the incorporated carbon dot with abundant functional groups of ─OH and ─NH<sub>2</sub> can facilitate rapid decomposing of NaPF<sub>6</sub> as well as accelerate NaF deposition kinetics on NVP surface to form a thin and stable NaF-rich CEI layer, greatly boosting its interfacial sodium-ion diffusion kinetics. As a consequence, charge transfer kinetics of NVP can be significantly boosted via a facile trace carbon incorporation approach, and the as-developed NVP/carbon dot (NVP/NCDs) cathode delivers superior performances with highly reversible capacity (≈112.3&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.5&nbsp;C with an initial coulombic efficiency of 97.3%), excellent rate performance (up to 200&nbsp;C), superfast charging property (≈6.5&nbsp;s per charging with specific capacity of 52.0&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> input), and stable cycling performance (98.4% capacity retention after 20&nbsp;k cycles). In addition, a full cell based on the as-developed NVP/NCDs cathode and hard carbon anode were developed, exhibiting a high energy density of 368.7&nbsp;Wh&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup> under power density of 314&nbsp;W&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup>, and fast charging feature of ≈110.2&nbsp;s per charging with 250.8&nbsp;Wh&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup> input.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202400595-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/9a8185cf-5d48-4dc9-ad41-d0e00120cbc5/aenm202400595-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/9a8185cf-5d48-4dc9-ad41-d0e00120cbc5/aenm202400595-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/ac5aa6bc-0876-4982-b6ad-455e9c7dfc20/aenm202400595-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/9a8185cf-5d48-4dc9-ad41-d0e00120cbc5/aenm202400595-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202400595-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202400595" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Illustration of optimization mechanism for carbon dot incorporation on NVP. a) SEM of NVP b) and NVP/NCDs. c) HRTEM of annealed NCDs d) and NVP/NCDs. e) STEM of NVP/NCDs f) with corresponding EDX element mapping images g).</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202400595-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202400595-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>NVP, carbon coated NVP (NVP/C) and NVP/NCDs were synthesized through a facile co-precipitation approach except for whether introducing traditional carbon source (glucose) or carbon dot, and their formation processes are detailed in Supporting Information. Morphologies of the NVP (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400595-fig-0001">1b</a>), NVP/C (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information) and NVP/NCDs (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400595-fig-0001">1c</a>) were investigated by scanning electron microscopy (SEM). Obviously, particle size of NVP is reduced and becomes more uniform mainly due to its inhibited particle-growth process induced by incorporated carbon dot (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400595-fig-0001">1d</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information) with positive ζ potential of 4.54&nbsp;mV. Furthermore, crystalline carbon dot decorated in amorphous carbon region with thickness of ≈7&nbsp;nm is observed on the surface of NVP (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400595-fig-0001">1e</a>) through transmission electron microscopy (TEM), indicating the successful carbon dot incorporation. Then, uniform element distributions in NVP/NCDs (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400595-fig-0001">1f,g</a>) and NVP (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information) were confirmed via employing energy dispersive X-ray (EDX) analysis coupling with scanning transmission electron microscope (STEM).</p>
         
         <p>IV-type N<sub>2</sub> adsorption–desorption isotherms of NVP, NVP/C, and NVP/NCDs with mesoporous structure were tested (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400595-fig-0002">2a</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400595-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400595-bib-0028_R_d174338181e747" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - ACS Nano">28</a>-<a href="#aenm202400595-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400595-bib-0030_R_d174338181e747" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Chem. Eng. J.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and specific surface areas of 2.5, 8.6, and 17.5&nbsp;m<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> are determined for the NVP, NVP/C and NVP/NCDs, respectively. The slightly increased specific surface area is ascribed to the reduced particle size caused by carbon dot incorporation. To investigate crystal structure evolution of NVP after traditional carbon coating and novel carbon dot incorporation, X-ray diffraction (XRD; Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400595-fig-0002">2b</a>) along with the refined analyses (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400595-fig-0002">2c</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information) were performed. The results exhibit pure NASICON structure of all samples with low <i>Chi<sup>2</sup></i> values less than 5%.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400595-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400595-bib-0031_R_d174338181e771" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - ACS Energy Lett.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Besides, lattice parameters and cell volume of all samples are similar (Tables <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>–<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information), indicating the unchanged phase structure of NVP after carbon modification. Furthermore, Raman was used to detect structure feature of the incorporated carbon on NVP/C and NVP/NCDs (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400595-fig-0002">2d,e</a>), in which two broad peaks can be deconvoluted into four parts.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400595-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400595-bib-0032_R_d174338181e786" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - J. Power Sources">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The bands located at ≈1340 and 1590&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> belong to sp<sup>2</sup>-type carbon, and the others at ≈1200 and 1510&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> are associated with sp<sup>3</sup>-type carbon. It was reported that integral area ratio of sp<sup>3</sup> to sp<sup>2</sup> (Asp<sup>3</sup>/Asp<sup>2</sup>) is highly related to graphitization degree.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400595-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400595-bib-0032_R_d174338181e808" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - J. Power Sources">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As could be compared in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400595-fig-0002">2e</a>, the Asp<sup>3</sup>/Asp<sup>2</sup> ratio of NVP/NCDs is smaller than that of NVP/C, indicating higher graphitization degree of surface carbon in NVP/NCDs, in favor of enhancing electrical conductivity of NVP. Then, carbon contents in NVP/C and NVP/NCDs are determined as 4.20 and 0.76&nbsp;wt.%, respectively, based on thermogravimetry analysis (TGA; Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400595-fig-0002">2f</a>).</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202400595-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/ff53f39d-4cef-4e8a-8796-08be47e9a9c2/aenm202400595-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/ff53f39d-4cef-4e8a-8796-08be47e9a9c2/aenm202400595-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/c5e902bc-6182-4956-acd1-6648cd5e25a6/aenm202400595-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/ff53f39d-4cef-4e8a-8796-08be47e9a9c2/aenm202400595-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202400595-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202400595" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">N<sub>2</sub> adsorption–desorption isotherm a) and XRD patterns. b) Rietveld refinement profiles of NVP/NCDs. c) Raman spectra, d) and derived Asp<sup>3</sup>/Asp<sup>2</sup> contributions of NVP/C and NVP/NCDs. e) TGA f) and FTIR spectra g) of all samples. C 1s h) and N 1s i) spectra of NVP/C and NVP/NCDs.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>To investigate surface functional groups, Fourier transform infrared (FTIR) analysis was conducted. As could be seen in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400595-fig-0002">2g</a>, peaks at 1186, 1055, and 632&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> belong to stretching bonds of PO<sub>4</sub> tetrahedron, signals at 632 and 578&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> are related to V<sup>3+</sup>─O<sup>2−</sup> bond, and peaks at 1720, 1570, and 1388&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> belong to surface C═C, C═O, and C═N, respectively. Notably, peak at 3687&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> is associated with O─H stretching vibration, and that on NVP/NCDs surface is more abundant in comparison to other samples. In addition, signal appeared at 3134&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> in NVP/NCDs is indexed to N─H stretching vibrations. The abundant functional groups of ─OH and ─NH<sub>2</sub> on the surface of NVP/NCDs is originated from the introduced carbon dot, possibly prompting interfacial kinetics of NVP. Subsequently, X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS; Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400595-fig-0002">2h,i</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information) was used to detect chemical environment of all samples. As could be compared in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400595-fig-0002">2h,i</a>, N 1s signal is observed in NVP/NCDs mainly owing to carbon dot with N doping, and it was reported that N doping could enhance charge transfer kinetics of carbons.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400595-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400595-bib-0033_R_d174338181e885" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Natl. Sci. Rev.">33</a>-<a href="#aenm202400595-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400595-bib-0035_R_d174338181e885" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Carbon">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Besides, more obvious signal at 288.8&nbsp;eV assigning to ─C═O/N─C═O could be observed in NVP/NCDs, further demonstrating abundant functional groups on NVP/NCDs, consistent with the FTIR results.</p>
         
         <p>Electrochemical performances of all samples as SIB cathodes in half-cell were investigated (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400595-fig-0003">3</a>). CV profiles of NVP, NVP/C and NVP/NCDs during initial four cycles are displayed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400595-fig-0003">3a</a>, and a pair of redox peaks ≈3.4&nbsp;V can be observed, indicating a reversible phase transformation between NVP and NaV<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> with two-electron transfer, which is further indicated through in situ XRD (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information). In addition, evolution for lattice parameter of NVP and NVP/NCDs derived from in situ XRD was compared (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a>, Supporting Information), and a slighter structure change is demonstrated in NVP/NCDs versus NVP, indicating the more stable structure of NVP during (de)sodiation after carbon dot incorporation. Notably, electrochemical polarization of NVP is significantly mitigated after carbon dot incorporation, which could be observed in CV curves (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400595-fig-0003">3a</a>), and charge–discharge profiles after different cycles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400595-fig-0003">3b</a>) as well as under different cycling rates (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400595-fig-0003">3c</a>). Rate performances of all samples are compared in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400595-fig-0003">3d</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a> (Supporting Information), and the NVP/NCDs exhibits best performances among them, delivering highly reversible capacities of 113.9, 113.0, 110.9, 108.9, 104.1, 86.9, 78.5, 69.6, and 66.7&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> under rates of 1, 2, 5, 10, 20, 50, 70, 90, and 100&nbsp;C, respectively. In addition, cycling performances of NVP, NVP/C and NVP/NCDs under 0.5&nbsp;C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400595-fig-0003">3b</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>, Supporting Information), 5&nbsp;C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400595-fig-0003">3e</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>, Supporting Information), and 50&nbsp;C (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>, Supporting Information) were compared, in which the NVP/NCDs exhibits most stable cycling performances among them. For example, 95.4% capacity retention after 400 cycles could be retained in NVP/NCDs under 5 C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400595-fig-0003">3e</a>), which is much better than that in NVP/C (56.3%) and NVP (23.7%). Besides, initial coulombic efficiency of 97.3% is exhibited in NVP/NCDs which is higher than that in NVP/C (96.1%) and NVP (86.3%). In addition, it is noted that the electrochemical performances of NVP/NCDs are highly related with the incorporated carbon dot contents (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a>, Supporting Information), and the electrochemical activity along with electrochemical performances of NVP are reduced when excess carbon dot content is introduced. To further evaluate the performance potential of NVP/NCDs, its cycling performance at ultrahigh rate of 200&nbsp;C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400595-fig-0003">3f</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a>, Supporting Information) was conducted. Remarkably, it shows a fast-charging property of ≈6.5&nbsp;s per charging with specific capacity of ≈52.0&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> input and high capacity retention of ≈98.4% after stabilization process during 20&nbsp;k cycles. Its electrochemical performances especially for cycling and rate performances transcend most of other reported NVP cathodes (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400595-fig-0003">3g</a>; and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400595-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400595-bib-0022_R_d174338181e969" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">22</a>, <a href="#aenm202400595-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400595-bib-0036_R_d174338181e969" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Chem. Eng. J.">36</a>-<a href="#aenm202400595-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400595-bib-0048_R_d174338181e969" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">48</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, the NVP/NCDs based half-cell exhibits the lowest self-discharge rate among all samples (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a>, Supporting Information) mainly due to the improved electrochemical kinetics induced by carbon dot modification. Furthermore, electrochemical performances of NVP/NCDs with high active mass loading (4.5, 5.2, and 8.0&nbsp;mg&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>) and tap density of 2.5–3&nbsp;g&nbsp;cm<sup>−3</sup> have been evaluated. As could be observed in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a> (Supporting Information), the NVP/NCDs exhibits high capacity, excellent rate performance, and stable cycling with different mass loadings. For example, reversible capacities of 87.6, 84.5, 80.9, and 76.7&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 1, 5, 10, and 20&nbsp;C could be retained in NVP/NCDs with active mass loading of 8.0&nbsp;mg&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>, respectively. When further cycling at active mass loading of 8.0&nbsp;mg&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>, ultrahigh capacity retention of 99.3% is exhibited after 500 cycles for NVP/NCDs, indicating its great potential for further applications.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202400595-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/20cdb8cd-5261-454c-b96e-4c9876e27611/aenm202400595-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/20cdb8cd-5261-454c-b96e-4c9876e27611/aenm202400595-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/edcabdd4-e0c1-419b-948c-53c90f460347/aenm202400595-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/20cdb8cd-5261-454c-b96e-4c9876e27611/aenm202400595-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202400595-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202400595" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">CV profiles under 0.2&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>, a) and charge–discharge curves after the different cycles under 0.5 C b) as well as under different rates c) of all samples. Rate, d) and cycling performances e) of all samples. Cycling performance of NVP/NCDs under 200&nbsp;C. f) Electrochemical performance comparison among NVP/NCDs and other reported NVP cathodes, g) where HR means the reported highest rate and SCL is specific capacity at low rate.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>To investigate optimization mechanism of electrochemical performances for NVP induced by carbon dot incorporation, electrochemical kinetics of NVP, NVP/C, and NVP/NCDs was investigated (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400595-fig-0004">4</a>). First, cyclic voltammetry (CV) profiles of all samples after 100 cycles under the different scan rates are compared in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400595-fig-0004">4a–c</a>. Large electrochemical polarization of NVP (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400595-fig-0004">4a</a>) and NVP/C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400595-fig-0004">4b</a>) is exhibited, and that of NVP/NCDs could be greatly restrained after carbon dot incorporation (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400595-fig-0004">4c</a>). In addition, sodium-ion diffusion coefficient could be calculated based on the CV curves under different scan rates (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400595-fig-0004">4d</a>; Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information)<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400595-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400595-bib-0049_R_d174338181e1041" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - J. Solid State Electrochem.">49</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT; Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400595-fig-0004">4e,f</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a>, Supporting Information),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400595-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400595-bib-0050_R_d174338181e1053" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">50</a>-<a href="#aenm202400595-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400595-bib-0052_R_d174338181e1053" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">52</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> respectively. The sodium-ion diffusion coefficient of NVP/NCDs is much higher than that of NVP and NVP/C, confirming the much-improved electrochemical kinetics of NVP/NCDs after carbon dot incorporation. Furthermore, electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) spectra of NVP (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18a</a>, Supporting Information), NVP/C (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18b</a>, Supporting Information), and NVP/NCDs (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400595-fig-0004">4g</a>) after the different cycles were investigated to compare interfacial charge transfer,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400595-bib-0053" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400595-bib-0053_R_d174338181e1068" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 53 - ACS Nano">53</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and the NVP/NCDs shows much lower R<sub>CEI</sub> and R<sub>ct</sub> values versus NVP and NVP/C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400595-fig-0004">4h</a>; and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information), verifying the significantly improved interfacial charge transfer kinetics of NVP/NCDs after carbon dot modification. Last, normalized imaginary part versus frequency derived from Nyquist plots is displayed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400595-fig-0004">4i</a>, and operating frequency (f<sub>0</sub>) as well as characteristic relaxation time constant (τ<sub>o</sub>) are important quantitative indicators to indicate how fast the electrode materials can be charged–discharged reversibly.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400595-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400595-bib-0054_R_d174338181e1091" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 54 - Nano Energy">54</a>, <a href="#aenm202400595-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400595-bib-0055_R_d174338181e1091" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 55 - Adv. Mater.">55</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The NVP/NCDs exhibits a highest f<sub>0</sub> value among all samples, unambiguously demonstrating boosted charge transfer kinetics of NVP/NCDs.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202400595-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/f366231b-3789-48b1-b17c-a61e258f7613/aenm202400595-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/f366231b-3789-48b1-b17c-a61e258f7613/aenm202400595-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/3a01c28e-92fd-4089-82f6-7df21ed8c3e1/aenm202400595-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/f366231b-3789-48b1-b17c-a61e258f7613/aenm202400595-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202400595-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202400595" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">CV profiles of NVP, a) NVP/C, b) and NVP/NCDs c) after 100 cycles under various scan rates. Linear relation between square root of the scan rates (v<sup>1/2</sup>) and peak currents (I<sub>p</sub>). d) Sodium-ion diffusion coefficient obtained from GITT curves of NVP, NVP/C, and NVP/NCDs during charging e) and discharging. f) Nyquist plots of NVP/NCDs after different cycles. g) R<sub>CEI</sub> and R<sub>ct</sub> of NVP, NVP/C, and NVP/NCDs derived from the Nyquist plots. h) Normalized imaginary parts versus frequency derived from Nyquist plots i).</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>To gain in-depth insight into the dynamics of sodium-ion diffusion across interface, CEI structures on NVP (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19a</a>, Supporting Information), NVP/C (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400595-fig-0005">5a</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19b</a>, Supporting Information), and NVP/NCDs (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400595-fig-0005">5b</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19c</a>, Supporting Information) were studied. After 1000 cycles, a thin and uniform CEI film merged with coating layer (8–15&nbsp;nm) remains on the NVP/NCDs surface (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400595-fig-0005">5b</a>), which is beneficial for fast interfacial sodium-ion diffusion, prompting the superior rate capability of NVP/NCDs. Moreover, the CEI layer shows more lattice fringes of NaF on NVP/NCDs in comparison to other samples (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19a–c</a>, Supporting Information), and the NaF presents lower sodium-ion diffusion barrier energies than organic components, contributing to improved interface sodium-ion diffusion kinetics and mechanical strength of CEI layer.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400595-bib-0056" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400595-bib-0056_R_d174338181e1154" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 56 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">56</a>-<a href="#aenm202400595-bib-0058" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400595-bib-0058_R_d174338181e1154" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 58 - Nat. Commun.">58</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In comparison, thicker and uneven CEI layers (12–80&nbsp;nm) are observed on the surface of NVP/C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400595-fig-0005">5a</a>) accompanied with more electrolyte decomposition. Afterward, compositions of the CEI layer were analyzed by XPS spectra with the different etching depths (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400595-fig-0005">5c–f</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19d–g</a>, Supporting Information). C, F, Na, and O signals were investigated at the different etching depths, and the corresponding atomic percentages are compared in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400595-fig-0005">5c,d</a>. Obviously, C content in the CEI structure of NVP/C is much higher than that in NVP/NCDs, affirming higher organic species contents in NVP/C versus NVP/NCDs. As could be compared in F element, its atomic percentage in NVP/C is much lower than that of NVP/NCDs, indicating the content of F-based species is much higher in the CEI layer of NVP/NCDs in comparison to NVP/C. This phenomenon demonstrates that abundant functional groups of ─OH and ─NH<sub>2</sub> as well as low carbon content on NVP/NCDs surface endowed by the introduced carbon dot may promote the NaPF<sub>6</sub> salt decomposition to generate more F-based compounds. Furthermore, the high-resolution F 1s spectra of NVP/C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400595-fig-0005">5e</a>) and NVP/NCDs (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400595-fig-0005">5f</a>) are compared, in which peaks at 684.7 (Na─F) and 687.4&nbsp;eV (P─F) are indexed to inorganic NaF and PO<sub>x</sub>F<sub>y</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400595-bib-0059" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400595-bib-0059_R_d174338181e1187" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 59 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">59</a>-<a href="#aenm202400595-bib-0062" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400595-bib-0062_R_d174338181e1187" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 62 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">62</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> respectively, owing to the NaPF<sub>6</sub> decomposition. Notably, the PO<sub>x</sub>F<sub>y</sub> proportion in the CEI film of NVP/NCDs is obviously lower than that in NVP/C at the different sputtering depths, affirming NaF is the main inorganic F-based component in the CEI film of NVP/NCDs, as well as demonstrating the higher NaF proportion in CEI film of NVP/NCDs versus NVP/C. The NaF-rich components in the CEI structure of NVP/NCDs induced by carbon dot incorporation can enable a dense, uniform and stable CEI film,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400595-bib-0063" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400595-bib-0063_R_d174338181e1199" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 63 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">63</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> accounting for the significantly enhanced charge transfer kinetics along with much improved cycling and rate performances of NVP/NCDs.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202400595-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/dddbc54c-62b9-4e4c-b8ba-d5beefc69d89/aenm202400595-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/dddbc54c-62b9-4e4c-b8ba-d5beefc69d89/aenm202400595-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/fa937ac4-f154-40ba-8295-57983af3fc0e/aenm202400595-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/dddbc54c-62b9-4e4c-b8ba-d5beefc69d89/aenm202400595-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202400595-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202400595" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">TEM and EDX element mapping images of NVP/C a) and NVP/NCDs b) after 1000 cycles. Atomic ratio of C, F, Na, and O after different etching time on NVP/C c) and NVP/NCDs. d) F 1s spectra of NVP/C e) and NVP/NCDs f) at the different sputtering depths. P─F bond dissociation energy on NVP, NVP/C, and NVP/NCDs. g) Binding energies of NaPF<sub>6</sub> and DME molecular on NVP, NVP/C, and NVP/NCDs. h) Binding energies of NaF on NVP, NVP/C, and NVP/NCDs i).</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>To deeply understand the effect of functional groups (─OH and ─NH<sub>2</sub>) from carbon dot on CEI structure formation, density functional theory (DFT) calculations were employed. First, dissociation processes of NaPF<sub>6</sub> on NVP, NVP/C, and NVP/NCDs were simulated, respectively (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20</a>, Supporting Information). As a comparison, the dissociation energy barrier of P─F bond breaking on NVP/NCDs is obviously lower than that on NVP and NVP/C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400595-fig-0005">5g</a>), significantly prompting decomposition of NaPF<sub>6</sub> on NVP/NCDs surface, as well as facilitating coupling between sodium ions and F anions. Furthermore, binding energies of NaPF<sub>6</sub>, DME molecular and NaF on NVP, NVP/C, and NVP/NCDs surfaces were simulated (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21</a>, Supporting Information), respectively, and the binding energies of those on NVP/NCDs are highest among all samples (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400595-fig-0005">5h,i</a>), in favor of rapid nucleation and growth of NaF layer on NVP/NCDs surface, as well as effectively blocking DME decomposition with a resultant thin and uniform NaF-rich CEI film.</p>
         
         <p>A full cell based on NVP/NCDs cathode and hard carbon anode (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S22</a>, Supporting Information) was then developed (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400595-fig-0006">6</a>). Charge–discharge potential platform of 3.3V is observed in full cell (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400595-fig-0006">6a</a>). A highly reversible capacity of 91.2&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.5&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> is maintained after 200 cycles with 81.3% capacity retention (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400595-fig-0006">6b</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S23</a>, Supporting Information). When testing under the different rates, specific capacities of 117.2, 112.7, 107.9, 104.5, 99.4, 98.1, and 91.9&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> under current densities of 0.1, 0.3, 0.7, 1, 1.5, 2, and 3&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> are exhibited (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400595-fig-0006">6c</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S24</a>, Supporting Information), respectively, demonstrating its excellent rate capability. Notably, high energy densities of 368.7&nbsp;Wh&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup> could be delivered under power density of 314&nbsp;W&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400595-fig-0006">6d</a>). When operating at the ultrahigh power density of 8190&nbsp;W&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup>, a high energy density of 250.8&nbsp;Wh&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup> is displayed with fast charging time of ≈110.2&nbsp;s (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400595-fig-0006">6e</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S24</a>, Supporting Information). With the superior electrochemical performances, a blue LED could be powered, affirming its practicality (inset in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400595-fig-0006">6d</a>). Notably, great feasibility of this trace carbon incorporation strategy has been broadened in designing other advanced cathodes, such as Na<sub>3</sub>(VO)<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F with higher operating potential versus NVP. As could be seen in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S25</a> (Supporting Information), cycling and rate performances of Na<sub>3</sub>(VO)<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F are significantly boosted after carbon dot incorporation.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202400595-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/d9353bcf-e057-4ccb-93f7-40808b78e9ae/aenm202400595-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/d9353bcf-e057-4ccb-93f7-40808b78e9ae/aenm202400595-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/1dcf636a-5029-48ae-9836-5e9fc6851b02/aenm202400595-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/d9353bcf-e057-4ccb-93f7-40808b78e9ae/aenm202400595-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202400595-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202400595" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">The dQ/dV curves of NVP/NCDs cathode and hard carbon anode. a) Cycling performance of full cell at 0.5&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. b) Rate capability, c) calculated energy/power densities (based on mass of cathode) d) and corresponding time-voltage profiles e) of full cell.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202400595-sec-0030">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202400595-sec-0030-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, a trace carbon incorporation strategy has been proposed, successfully unlocking the charge transfer limitation in NVP, as well as developing an advanced NVP/NCDs cathode with superior electrochemical performances. In addition, comprehensive understanding behind the effective strategy on boosting electrochemical performances of NVP cathode have been clarified based on in-depth research of electrochemical kinetics analyses, structure evolution, CEI structure investigation, and DFT calculation. First, particle size of NVP is reduced by carbon dot incorporation, in favor of shortening ion-diffusion path to accelerate diffusion kinetics. Second, electrical conductivity of NVP is greatly enhanced without sacrificing its high electrochemical activity due to the trace carbon coating. Third, rapid decomposing of NaPF<sub>6</sub> as well as fast nucleation and growth of NaF layer on NVP/NCDs surface can be achieved due to the introduced abundant functional groups of -OH and -NH<sub>2</sub> from carbon dot, contributing to construction of a thin, uniform and stable NaF-rich CEI film on NVP/NCDs, significantly improving its interfacial sodium-ion diffusion kinetics. Prospectively, effectiveness of the facile carbon dot incorporation tactic shows great potential to be broadened in developing other electrode materials with fast charging feature.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
